# this script should generally mirror in function the MVPA 2017 analysis being done with Jamil for 
# controllable/uncontrollable failure and persist/switch decisions
# Instead of that data we'll run a proof of concept on the Haxby 2011 data creating living/non-living labes
# and then subgroups based on original labels
from nilearn import datasets
data=[]
# By default 2nd subject will be fetched
haxby_dataset = datasets.fetch_haxby(n_subjects=6)

# print basic information on the dataset
print('Mask nifti image (3D) is located at: %s' % haxby_dataset.mask)
print('Functional nifti image (4D) is located at: %s' %
      haxby_dataset.func[0])

Mask nifti image (3D) is located at: /home/jdennison/nilearn_data/haxby2001/mask.nii.gz
Functional nifti image (4D) is located at: /home/jdennison/nilearn_data/haxby2001/subj1/bold.nii.gz

In [2]:
import numpy as np

for sub in [0,1,2,3,4,5]:
    # Load target information as string and give a numerical identifier to each
    behavioral = np.recfromcsv(haxby_dataset.session_target[sub], delimiter=" ")
    conditions = behavioral['labels']

    # Restrict the analysis to faces and places
    condition_mask = np.logical_or.reduce((conditions == b'face', conditions == b'house',conditions == b'cat',conditions == b'shoe',))
    conditions = conditions[condition_mask]
    tr_con=np.zeros(conditions.shape)
    #train on living=1 and non-living =2
    tr_con[np.logical_or(conditions=='face',conditions=='cat')]=1
    tr_con[np.logical_or(conditions=='shoe',conditions=='house')]=2


    # We now have 2 conditions
    print(np.unique(conditions))
    print tr_con
    session = behavioral[condition_mask]


    from nilearn.input_data import NiftiMasker

    mask_filename = haxby_dataset.mask

    # For decoding, standardizing is often very important
    # note that we are also smoothing the data
    masker = NiftiMasker(mask_img=mask_filename, smoothing_fwhm=4,
                         standardize=True, memory="nilearn_cache", memory_level=1)
    func_filename = haxby_dataset.func[sub]
    X = masker.fit_transform(func_filename)
    # Apply our condition_mask
    X = X[condition_mask]


    from sklearn.svm import SVC
    svc = SVC(kernel='linear',C=1)

    # Define the dimension reduction to be used.
    # Here we use a classical univariate feature selection based on F-test,
    # namely Anova. When doing full-brain analysis, it is better to use
    # SelectPercentile, keeping 5% of voxels
    # (because it is independent of the resolution of the data).
    from sklearn.feature_selection import SelectPercentile, f_classif
    feature_selection = SelectPercentile(f_classif, percentile=5)
    
    # We have our classifier (SVC), our feature selection (SelectPercentile),and now,
    # we can plug them together in a *pipeline* that performs the two operations
    # successively:
    from sklearn.pipeline import Pipeline
    anova_svc = Pipeline([('anova', feature_selection), ('svc', svc)])

    anova_svc.fit(X, tr_con)
    y_pred = anova_svc.predict(X)
    
    from sklearn.cross_validation import LeaveOneLabelOut, cross_val_score
    from sklearn.model_selection import KFold
    # Define the cross-validation scheme used for validation.
    # Here we use a LeaveOneLabelOut cross-validation on the session label
    # which corresponds to a leave-one-session-out
    cv = KFold(n_splits=6,shuffle=True)
    
    # Compute the prediction accuracy for the different folds (i.e. session)
    cv_scores = cross_val_score(anova_svc, X, tr_con,cv=6)
    
    # Return the corresponding mean prediction accuracy
    classification_accuracy = cv_scores.mean()
    
    # Print the results
    print("Classification accuracy: %.4f / Chance level: %f" %
          (classification_accuracy, 1. / len(np.unique(tr_con))))
    # Classification accuracy: 0.8009 / Chance level: 0.5000





    coef = svc.coef_
    # reverse feature selection
    coef = feature_selection.inverse_transform(coef)
    # reverse masking
    weight_img = masker.inverse_transform(coef)
    
    
    # Use the mean image as a background to avoid relying on anatomical data
    from nilearn import image
    mean_img = image.mean_img(func_filename)
    
    # Create the figure
    from nilearn.plotting import plot_stat_map, show
    plot_stat_map(weight_img, mean_img, title='SVM weights')
    
    # Saving the results as a Nifti file may also be important
    weight_img.to_filename('haxby_face_vs_house.nii')

    show()


    dist=np.zeros(tr_con.shape)
    for train_index, test_index in cv.split(X):
        print("TRAIN:", train_index, "TEST:", test_index)
        X_train, X_test = X[train_index], X[test_index]
        y_train, y_test = tr_con[train_index], tr_con[test_index]
        svc.fit(X_train,y_train)
        dist[test_index]=svc.decision_function(X[test_index])
        print dist
        print svc.decision_function(X[test_index])
        
    data.append([conditions,tr_con,dist])

one=np.hstack([data[0][0],data[1][0],data[2][0],data[3][0],data[4][0],data[5][0]])
one[one=='face']=1
one[one=='cat']=2
one[one=='shoe']=3
one[one=='house']=4
one = map(int, one)
one=pd.Series(one)
two=pd.Series(np.hstack([data[0][1],data[1][1],data[2][1],data[3][1],data[4][1],data[5][1]]))
three=pd.Series(np.hstack([data[0][2],data[1][2],data[2][2],data[3][2],data[4][2],data[5][2]]))
